        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c01894"><meta property="og:title" content="Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na3V2(PO4)2F3 Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/acsami.4c01894.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) has been regarded as a favorable cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high voltage and stable structure. However, the limited electronic conductivity restricts its rate performance. NVPF@reduced graphene oxide (rGO) was synthesized by a facile microwave-assisted hydrothermal approach with subsequent calcination to shorten the hydrothermal time. NVPF nanocuboids with sizes of 50–150 nm distributed on rGO can be obtained, delivering excellent electrochemical performance such as a longevity life (a high capacity retention of 85.6% after 7000 cycles at 10 C) and distinguished rate capability (116 mAh g–1 at 50 C with a short discharging/charging time of 1.2 min). The full battery with a Cu2Se anode represents a capacity of 116 mAh g–1 at 0.2 A g–1. The introduction of rGO can augment the electronic conductivity and advance the Na+ diffusion speed, boosting the cycling and rate capability. Besides, the small lattice change (3.3%) and high structural reversibility during the phase transition process between Na3V2(PO4)2F3 and NaV2(PO4)2F3 testified by in situ X-ray diffraction are also advantageous for Na storage behavior. This work furnishes a simple method to synthesize polyanionic cathodes with ultrahigh rate and ultralong lifespan for fast-charging SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/acsami.4c01894.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931883ccabf154b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883ccabf154b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'e16a0592-81ae-4c43-a0de-0cb875c7db37'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

img{border:0}.a2a_button_facebook_like iframe{max-width:none}</style><script charset="utf-8" async="" src="https://script.hotjar.com/modules.f98993dba31a932bc912.js"></script><script id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/vendors~lazy-imports-c0b82d66d33565b4cebe.js"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><script src="https://script.hotjar.com/browser-perf.8417c6bba72228fa2e29.js"></script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883ccabf154b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931883ccabf154b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Microwave-Assisted Hydrothermal Synthesis of Na&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;V&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;F&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocuboid@Reduced Graphene Oxide as an Ultrahigh-Rate and Superlong-Lifespan Cathode for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c01894", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                <a title="Abstract" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#Abstract" data-id="article_header_JumpTo_Abstract">
                   <span>Abstract</span>
                </a>
            </li>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">1</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/medium/am4c01894_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c01894&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) has been regarded as a favorable cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high voltage and stable structure. However, the limited electronic conductivity restricts its rate performance. NVPF@reduced graphene oxide (rGO) was synthesized by a facile microwave-assisted hydrothermal approach with subsequent calcination to shorten the hydrothermal time. NVPF nanocuboids with sizes of 50–150 nm distributed on rGO can be obtained, delivering excellent electrochemical performance such as a longevity life (a high capacity retention of 85.6% after 7000 cycles at 10 C) and distinguished rate capability (116 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C with a short discharging/charging time of 1.2 min). The full battery with a Cu<sub>2</sub>Se anode represents a capacity of 116 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The introduction of rGO can augment the electronic conductivity and advance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion speed, boosting the cycling and rate capability. Besides, the small lattice change (3.3%) and high structural reversibility during the phase transition process between Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> and NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> testified by in situ X-ray diffraction are also advantageous for Na storage behavior. This work furnishes a simple method to synthesize polyanionic cathodes with ultrahigh rate and ultralong lifespan for fast-charging SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D20559%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJieduo%2BGuan%252C%2BShilin%2BZhou%252C%2BJiajie%2BZhou%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D16%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c01894%26title%3DMicrowave-Assisted%2BHydrothermal%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25292F3%2BNanocuboid%2540Reduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2Ban%2BUltrahigh-Rate%2Band%2BSuperlong-Lifespan%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BFast-Charging%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D20569%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fast-charging&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_fast-charging">fast-charging</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3v22f3%40rgo&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3V2%28PO4%292F3%40rGO">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>@rGO</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=In+Situ+XRD&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_in%20situ%20XRD">in situ XRD</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have earned much attention for large-scale energy storage on account of the affordable cost and plentiful supply of sodium resources. In comparison to lithium, sodium has a bigger ion radius (1.02 Å for Na<sup>+</sup> vs 0.72 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>) and heavier molar mass (22.99 g mol<sup>–1</sup> for Na vs 6.94 g mol<sup>–1</sup> for Li), causing tardy Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics during the sodiation/desodiation processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> This tardy Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion leads to a poor electrochemical performance. In recent years, many attempts have been dedicated to seeking suitable cathode materials with high power capability, long cycle life, and good safety, such as metal oxide, Prussian blue analogs, polyanionic compounds, organic materials, and fluorides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 6 to 13">(6−13)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Among them, sodium superionic conductor (NASICON)-type phosphates possess a stable 3D open framework structure, which connects by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedrons and MO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons (M represents a transition metal). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> This structure ensures enough space for the transfer and storage of Na<sup>+</sup> and buffers volume change throughout the repetitive cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Particularly, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) has been regarded a research hotspot in cathode fields owing to its high capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and high average operating potential of 4.0 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The theoretical energy density can reach 500 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, which is close to commercial lithium cathodes, such as LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (480 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) and LiFePO<sub>4</sub> (530 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> However, the low electronic conductivity (10<sup>–12</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>) limits its cycling and rate properties, especially at high rates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> Coating with carbon-based materials, constructing nanostructure, and doping elements are the common modified strategies to improve Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and electronic conductivity, bringing out good cyclability and rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> For instance, carbon coating can improve electronic transport. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> Zhang’s team designed NVPF particles secured on N-doped carbon nanotubes, which exhibited capacities of 126 and 76 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 and 100 C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Constructing a nanostructure can reduce the ionic/electronic diffusion path and augment the interfacial contact between an electrode and an electrolyte. Zhou et al. prepared well-dispersed NVPF nanoparticles coated by reduced graphene oxide (rGO) with capacities of 123 and 95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 and 30 C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Wu’s group utilized lattice regulation of Mn<sup>2+</sup> for V<sup>3+</sup> to strengthen the intrinsic electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics, delivering capacities of 123.8 and 83.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 and 20 C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Although the performance at low current density has been modified by these strategies, the high rate property remains suboptimal to fulfill commercial demands for fast-charging SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The familiar preparation method for NVPF is a hydrothermal method with follow-up calcination treatment, especially for NVPF with high crystallinity and special nanostructure, which generally needs a long time. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 25 and 33 to 35">(25,33−35)</a> Compared to the conventional hydrothermal approach, the microwave-assisted hydrothermal method equips the preponderances of economized energy, high efficiency, short time, homogeneous heating, and easy controllability, regarding as the underlying synthesis approach. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> Moreover, microwave irradiation can infiltrate and heat entire raw materials at the same time, resulting in more uniform nanoparticles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Several research groups have already employed this technique to prepare NVPF-based cathodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> However, further improvements are needed in the optimization of energy-saving preparation and sodium storage capability, especially in terms of the rate capacity and endurance life.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Hence, in this work, we have prepared NVPF nanocuboids coated with rGO by a microwave-assisted hydrothermal method with postcalcination. NVPF@rGO can be obtained under different experimental conditions including microwave hydrothermal temperatures (120–140 °C), microwave hydrothermal times (0.5–2 h), calcination times (4–10 h), calcination temperatures (500–600 °C), and rGO addition (8–17%). NVPF nanocuboids with sizes of 50–150 nm are evenly decentralized on the rGO, showing the best electrochemical properties. For example, a high reversible capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 84% at 1 C can be obtained. After 7000 cycles, a steady capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high capacity conservation rate of 85.6% is attained at 10 C. A high capacity of 116 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the superhigh rate of 50 C is gained, signifying that one charging time is only 1.2 min. The performance of a full battery composed of NVPF@rGO as the cathode and commercial Cu<sub>2</sub>Se as the anode is also explored. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and cyclic voltammetry (CV) reveal that the enhanced reaction kinetics, including a small resistance, high pseudocapacitive contribution, and big Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient due to the introduction of rGO are beneficial to the preeminent properties. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) consequences demonstrate a highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction mechanism with a low volume change of 3.3% for NVPF@rGO.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Sample Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">V(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was dissolved in absolute ethanol at ambient temperature, stirring for 0.5 h. Meanwhile, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and NaF were also dissolved in water under the same conditions. Then graphene oxide (GO) solution was mixed with the two solutions, stirred for 0.5 h, transferred into a 100 mL microwave hydrothermal autoclave, and maintained at 130 °C for 1 h. The product was vacuum filtered and washed thoroughly with deionized water and ethanol, and then dried at 80 °C. The sediment was calcined at 600 °C for 7 h under N<sub>2</sub>. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> was prepared by the same process without GO. The structure and electrochemical characterization were added in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a represents the synthetic process of NVPF@rGO. In brief, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, V(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and NaF were dissolved in a hybrid solution of absolute ethanol and deionized water (v: v= 2:1). Then GO solution was added into this solution and transferred to a microwave hydrothermal autoclave and microwave-heated at a certain temperature for some times. The washed and dried sediment was calcined at different temperatures under N<sub>2</sub> to obtain the target product. The morphologies of NVPF@rGO and NVPF were probed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). Compared to the irregular nanoparticles of pure NVPF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), NVPF@rGO consists of regular nanocuboids with sizes of 50–150 nm, equally dispersing on the rGO sheets exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. The distinct lattice fringes with a width of 0.32 nm are attributable to the (220) plane of the NVPF phase in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, showing good crystallinity. As displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, the (107), (200), and (222) crystal faces are also clearly observed in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) image. These results demonstrate that the introduction of GO is beneficial to the formation of high crystallinity and a regular structure in the synthetic process. The energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) images evidence the well-proportioned distribution of Na, V, P, O, F, and C in the nanocuboid structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e), suggesting that nanocuboid Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/rGO can be resoundingly obtained via the microwave-assisted hydrothermal approach with the subsequent calcination treatment.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/medium/am4c01894_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic preparation of NVPF@rGO. Morphological analysis of NVPF@rGO, (b) SEM, (c) TEM, (d) HRTEM, and (e) EDX images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c01894&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The purity of NVPF@rGO is influenced by experimental conditions including the temperatures and times of the microwave hydrothermal method and the subsequent calcination. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>a, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,b, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4–S8</a> reveal that pure NVPF@rGO can be acquired at various microwave hydrothermal temperatures (120–140 °C), microwave hydrothermal times (0.5–2 h), calcination times (4–10 h), calcination temperatures (500–600 °C), and rGO addition (8–17%). Once the calcination temperature reaches 700 °C, NVPF@rGO decomposes and transforms to Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and other compounds. The influence of these factors on the electrochemical properties will be mentioned later. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b displays the Rietveld refinements of NVPF@rGO and NVPF, where the calculated curves are in keeping with the experimental raw patterns. The characteristic peaks are commendably assigned to the standard values of the tetragonal Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (PDF: 89-8485) with the space group of P42/mnm. The corresponding crystal structure is exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, where the bioctahedral [V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>F<sub>3</sub>] and tetrahedral [PO<sub>4</sub>] units build an open 3D framework for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, promoting the fast transmission of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Two different positions of Na<sup>+</sup> are the completely occupied site (Na1) and partly occupied site (Na2) in this framework. The former cations are not easy to transfer from the structure. The electrochemical reaction is commonly hinged on the insertion/extraction of Na2 cations due to the lower energy barrier. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/medium/am4c01894_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Rietveld refined XRD patterns of (a) NVPF@rGO and (b) NVPF. (c) Crystal structure of the NVPF. (d) Raman spectra of NVPF@rGO and NVPF. (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of NVPF@rGO and NVPF. (f) XPS V 2p spectrum of NVPF@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c01894&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">NVPF@rGO and NVPF electrodes were ulteriorly characterized by Raman spectroscopy in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d. The P–O symmetrical stretching vibration of PO<sub>4</sub> is responsible for the characteristic bands at 906 and 942 cm<sup>–1</sup>, while the P–O asymmetrical stretching vibration of PO<sub>4</sub> is answerable for the band at 1052 cm<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 44 to 47">(44−47)</a> The characteristic bands of rGO (D band at 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup>, G band at 1598 cm<sup>–1</sup>) can be detected in NVPF@rGO. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref48" aria-label="reference 25 and 48">(25,48)</a> <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is about 1.02, showing a similar degree of graphitized and disordered carbon. The rGO with high electrical conductivity and some defects enhances the electrochemical activity of NVPF. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curves were conducted to reveal the specific surface area of NVPF@rGO and NVPF shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e. NVPF@rGO equips a larger surface area (85.73 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) than that of NVPF (11.44 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). The pore size distribution diagrams display that NVPF and NVPF@rGO are mesoporous materials with pore sizes of about 2–10 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). The contact angle between the NVPF electrode and the electrolyte is 12.4°, while the electrolyte hardly forms significant droplets on the NVPF@rGO electrode in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, indicating that the NVPF@rGO electrode exhibits superior wettability. The augmented surface area and enhanced wettability facilitate the interfacial contact between the electrolyte and electrode, obtaining good electrochemical properties. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) test was performed to explore the valence states of NVPF@rGO. The peaks of Na 1s, P 2p, V 2p, O 1s, F 1s, and C 1s can be observed in the XPS survey profile (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Two distinguished peaks at 517.8 and 524.9 eV are associated with V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of V<sup>3+</sup> in NVPF@rGO, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). The actual carbon contents of these three samples are 7.5 (denoted as NVPF@8%rGO), 11.1 (denoted as NVPF@rGO), and 16.8% (denoted as NVPF@17%rGO) calculated from thermogravimetric (TG) curve in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>. The weight stabilizes after 480 °C, indicating the high thermostability of the NVPF.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical characteristics of NVPF@rGO and NVPF cathodes were examined by galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles and CV curves. The CV curves of NVPF@rGO at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 2.5 and 4.3 V are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. Two pairs of redox peaks at 3.62/3.58 V and 4.05/4.01 V belong to the desodiation/sodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> ↔ Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup>), and Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> ↔ NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>–</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> respectively. Notably, the four CV profiles maintain stability in terms of peak positions and intensities, demonstrating high reversibility and stability upon repeated cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b depicts the initial charge–discharge profiles of NVPF@rGO and NVPF electrodes at 1 C (1 C = 128 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The charge and discharge plateaus correspond to the redox peaks of the CV curves for NVPF@rGO. Remarkably, the potential gap between discharge and charge plateaus for NVPF@rGO is smaller than that for pure NVPF. NVPF@rGO represents a discharge capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency of 84% at 1 C, which is greater than the value of pure NVPF (84 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, a low Coulombic efficiency of 58%). After 50 cycles, the capacity of NVPF@rGO is held at 122 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity conservation ratio of 99% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). NVPF@rGO also exhibits superior rate characteristics with capacities of 122, 125, 124, 122, 120, 118, and 116 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30, and 50 C in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d, respectively. The charging time is only 1.2 min at 50 C, demonstrating that this material is highly suitable for fast-charging SIBs. Upon reversal of the rate to 1 C, the capacity stabilizes at 123 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, NVPF delivers discharge capacities of only 83, 77, 64, 53, 40, 30, 18, and 83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the same rates. Except for the high capacity, NVPF@rGO also maintains more defined charge/discharge plateaus compared to NVPF along with an increase of current rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f). Particularly, NVPF@rGO represents competitive rate properties to NVPF-based materials reported in the previous papers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref22 ref31 ref35 ref43 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref22 ref31 ref35 ref43 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 3, 22, 31, 35, 43 and 49 to 56">(3,22,31,35,43,49−56)</a> Apart from that, NVPF@rGO exhibits preeminent long-term durability at a high rate. NVPF@rGO achieves a capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high capacity conservation ratio of 85.6% (a capacity attenuation of 0.002% per cycle) after 7000 cycles at 10 C, which far exceeds the values of pure NVPF (38 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity conservation ratio of 77.6% after 4000 cycles, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h). According to the above data, it can be concluded that the NVPF coating with rGO can effectively improve its capacity, cycling stability, and rate characteristics. Moreover, compared to NVPF@rGO prepared at various rGO addition (8–17%), microwave hydrothermal temperatures (120–140 °C), microwave hydrothermal times (0.5–2 h), calcination times (4–10 h), and calcination temperatures (500–600 °C), NVPF@rGO prepared at the condition of microwave hydrothermal temperature with 130 °C, microwave hydrothermal time with 1 h, calcination temperature with 600 °C, calcination time with 7 h, and the rGO content of 11% exhibits the superior cycle and rate performance owing to its lowest charge transfer impedance (11.4 Ω) shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S13–S18 and Tables S3 and S4</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/medium/am4c01894_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) CV curves of NVPF@rGO at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) First charge/discharge profiles of NVPF@rGO and NVPF at 1 C. (c) Cycling performance at 1 C. (d) Rate performance of the NVPF@rGO and NVPF. (e, f) Charge/discharge profiles at different rates of NVPF@rGO and NVPF. (g) Performance comparison of NVPF-based materials. (h) Cycling performance of NVPF@rGO and NVPF at 10 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c01894&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To realize the reason for the difference in rate capability between NVPF@rGO and NVPF, CV experiments at different scanning rates (0.2–3 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) were performed to obtain the kinetic parameters shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,d. The oxidation peaks move to higher voltages as well as the reduction peaks move to lower voltages along with the increase in scanning rates. The voltage gap of A1/C1 for NVPF@rGO increases from 0.07 V at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> to 0.30 V at 3 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and the voltage gap of A2/C2 changes from 0.05 to 0.27 V. These values are considerably lower than the corresponding values (0.14 to 0.60 V for A1/C1 and 0.10 to 0.49 V for A2/C2) observed for NVPF, indicating that NVPF@rGO exhibits smaller voltage polarization and better rate performance. Currently, the following equations describe the relation between peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>):<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-5"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-6" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-7">i</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-8" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-9">p</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-10"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-37">log</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-38"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-40">ν</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-41" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-55"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-56"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-57" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-58">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-59" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-60">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-61">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-62" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-63"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-64" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-65">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-66" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-67">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-68"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-69" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-70">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-71" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-72">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-73">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-74">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="italic">i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div>where <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are parameters. The <i>b</i> value is close to 1, manifesting a pseudocapacitive process. As <i>b</i> approaches 0.5, the diffusion behavior takes control of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 57 to 60">(57−60)</a> The <i>b</i> values of A1, A2, C1, and C2 for NVPF@rGO are calculated to be 0.724, 0.822, 0.718, and 0.806, while these values for NVPF are 0.712, 0.858, 0.684, and 0.678 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>, respectively. These values lie between 0.5 and 1, demonstrating that the surface pseudocapacitive and diffusion-controlled behaviors jointly control the discharge/charge processes. The contribution ratio of pseudocapacitance can be quantified by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>. The pseudocapacitive contributions for NVPF@rGO are 92.7, 93.8, 94.6, 95.1, 96.7, and 97.9% at 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, 1, 2, and 3 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, surpassing that of NVPF shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b,e. The introduction of rGO not only aggrandizes the specific surface area of this electrode to accelerate the infiltration of electrolytes and enhance the surface pseudocapacitive Na storage behavior but also improves the electronic conductivity of this composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> The nanocuboid structure of NVPF can also abridge the sodium ion diffusion paths. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> Hence, these advantageous structural features of NVPF@rGO cause high pseudocapacitive behavior, especially at a high rate, bringing out the preeminent rate and cycling capability.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/medium/am4c01894_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0004.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV profiles at different scanning rates for (a) NVPF@rGO and (d) NVPF. Contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled capacity at different rates for (b) NVPF@rGO and (e) NVPF. Linear fitting of the current and the square root of the scanning rate for (c) NVPF@rGO and (f) NVPF. GITT profiles for (g) NVPF@rGO and (h) NVPF.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/asset/images/large/am4c01894_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c01894&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Besides, a cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) with a thickness of 3–4 nm on the surface of the material after charging can be observed (TEM, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>). XPS spectra were further applied to analyze the composition of this CEI in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>. In the C 1s spectrum, the peaks at 284.8 (C–C), 286.1 (C–O), 287.1 (C═O), 289.0 (O═C–OH), and 290.5 eV (−CF<sub>2</sub>−) correspond to the decomposition of the electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21a</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21b</a>, the F 1s signal after charging consists of −CF<sub>2</sub>–, Na–F, and V–F, where V–F and −CF<sub>2</sub>– are associated with NVPF and PVDF, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The appearance of the Na–F signal indicates that the electrolyte decomposes on the electrode surface to form inorganic NaF. The CEI containing inorganic components can effectively inhibit further electrolyte oxidation and electrode dissolution, thereby achieving stable cycling properties of the battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> EIS curves at different cycles also confirm the above conclusions in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c01894/suppl_file/am4c01894_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22 and Table S4</a>. The charge transfer impedance increases to 14.7 Ω after one cycle and tends to stabilize after 50 cycles due to the CEI.</div><div class="NLM_p">The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can also be obtained by the preceding CVs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,d). The linear relation between the square root of the scanning rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) and the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) from CVs is exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c,f. In the light of the Randles–Sevik equation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values for A1, A2, C1, and C2 of NVPF@rGO are calculated to be 8.572 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, 1.248 × 10<sup>–10</sup>, 7.103 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, and 9.048 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are far more than the relevant values of NVPF (3.064 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, 5.070 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, 2.231 × 10<sup>–11</sup>, and 1.102 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). These data reveal that combining NVPF with rGO can supply fast pathways for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Obviously, the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values of the A1/C1 redox peaks are lower than those of the A2/C2 redox peaks, illustrating that the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process at a low potential is more difficult and energy intensive. To further probe the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of NVPF@rGO and pure NVPF, GITT profiles at 0.5 C in the eighth cycle are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g,h. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> can be calculated using the following formula:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-75"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-76"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-77"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-78"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-79"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-80" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-81">D</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script"
